Chapter 40.
Which is the summary of the sermon regarding the appreciation of apostemes (abscesses) and their incision.
Apostemes (abscesses) are specific spots with various appearances depending on the stage they are at; each one is described in detail in this book.
And they are different in their treatment and incision due to two main reasons: the first is related to the type of aposteme itself, and the second depends on the amount of humidity they have.
The second way is determined by the location where the aposteme occurs in the body, as an abscess on the head is different from one found on the buttocks or from the one occurring at a joint. Each type of aposteme requires specific treatment and intervention.
Some types of abscesses should not be incised but rather allowed to mature until the pus drains naturally or be filled with medicine (ointment).
There are other abscesses that need to be pierced when they are hard and have not yet drained or softened, like the ones located close to a joint, as if you leave an abscess untreated next to a joint for too long it can cause the ligaments or nerves of said joint to get infected and become compromised. This leads to complications in movement by causing contractions in that limb. Another type of aposteme is one found on main limbs, which will damage the main limb itself if left untreated for too long before draining them; then you have the abscesses located next to or around the buttocks which should be drained when they are not yet fully developed (soft), and never pierced when hard because of the risk that this presents in damaging muscles and creating a fistula, or a cutaneous extension from said aposteme. Lastly, it is important to note that you should also not drain abscesses with poor digestion as this can lead to more complications and cause new apostemes. Mays cove que tu sapias la hora de la perforacio de la apostema, e aysso es quan es maturat.

On the treatment of ulcer.
An ulcer is when the womb of a woman is not perforated or the tear is small. And it is either natural or accidental.
It is accidental due to the illness that has preceded it, which is caused by either improperly joined flesh or subtle and thick pus.
It can occur in the region of the mother's kidney or on her sides, as well as above and below it. However, I forbid you to cauterize, burn, or treat with ointments, especially during menstruation.

However, those that are found outside the anus have less moisture; from them flows a small amount of yellow water or blood with continuous flow, and their colour matches that of the body.
To treat the hemorrhoids that are found inside the anus, instruct the patient to sit and squeeze themselves until the anus opens up and the haemorrhoids appear. Then cauterize them with hooks or grasp them with your nail, then cut them off at the root.
But if the hooks cannot be used on them because of their moisture and softness, then take them with a rough cloth or cotton or wool string, extract them using your aforementioned method, and then cut them at the root; dust some powder onto the wound after cutting to act as a cauterizing agent, or simply cauterize them as described in the chapter on cauterization. And then treat those with other cures until they are healed.
And if the tumor does not obey and expel [the content], then harden the patient's condition with a small cauterization, and wash it with that water from the well which can cause an easy expulsion when pressure is applied to the patient.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside of the anus, then their cause is light, and you can take them with your nail or a hook, extract them, and then treat them with what we have mentioned until they are healed.
And for those whom you favor to incise, know that you must administer their suture like this: take a thread wrapped around [a spindle] and pass it through the needle; then extract the hemorrhoids in two places above, and make the needle penetrate into the base of the other part; wrap the two ends of the thread on the place below the needle, longitudinally; tie the hemorrhoid with strong tension, and then knot the thread and remove the needle.
Do this to all of them, leaving one untreated and uncut, so that the excess blood may flow from it.

On bloodletting from veins:
The veins usually used for bloodletting are as follows: three from the head, described in chapter 16, two pulsatile veins located near the ears called 'alhasisain'; two herbs found on the temples; and two veins that run along the tears of two eyes, known as 'aspicientz'. There is also one vein prepared at the middle point of the forehead and another at the tip of the nose. Additionally, there are two points on the neck and two veins located above and below the lips. Finally, there are two more veins underneath the tongue; these last ones are called 'angelic' veins. Moreover, the veins that are bloodlet in the arms total five: one is the cephalic vein, also known as 'the wild arm', which the common people call 'the head vein'; another is a paralyzed vein, commonly referred to as 'the sleep vein'. This is joined by the median cubital vein, or what the locals call 'the body's vein', while others name it after its function: 'digestion vein' . It derives from one branch of the basilic and another from the cephalic. The latter is also known as "the arm's vein" in common parlance, whereas the former is referred to by some as 'abdominal vein'. There is also a vein called 'funis brachii', found over the elbow, which is frequently used for bloodletting due to its visible appearance on the pit; and finally, there is the 'salvatella' vein located between the thumb's tip and the smallest finger, presenting two branches.

Chapter Two: Concerning fractures of the skull.
The types of fractures are many, and their forms are diverse, with distinct causes. Some fractures occur due to the impact of a blunt object, which drives all the bone into the brain, just as when it hits a tree trunk; this type is known as 'ascial'. Or, the object pierces part of the bone, cuts through the flesh but does not penetrate deeply; this kind of fracture is called 'levatio absoluta'.
The damage from these two types of fractures can be extensive or minor, and there is also 'contritio' or crushing fracture, which results from being struck with a heavy object or falling onto rock; they are similar in appearance.
This fracture may be penetrating, almost reaching the membrane under the bone, or it could be on the surface of the bone; again, the extent of damage from these two types can vary.
There is also the fracture that occurs within the bone itself, along the fine threads of its structure; this type is known as 'capillar' or hairline fracture.

On restoring fractures in the foot.
The feet are sometimes broken in the middle, but their extremities may also be crushed and cut off.
During accidents when the feet are broken in the middle, it leads to swelling and pain within them, causing difficulty in walking and breathing, coughing, severe fatigue, vomiting blood, and an evident break in the bone that is not hidden from perception.
The process of restoration involves turning the patient onto their back and placing a cushion under their shoulders; then pressing on their muscles, adjusting their ribs with both hands, performing the operation delicately, and aligning all parts as best as possible according to necessity until the bone regains its original form.

On the restoration of fractures, when it is restored but this remains a member delicate and feeble not according to its nature."
When a bone fracture is healed, but the limb remains delicate and weak, it can be attributed to several factors: one reason could be an excess of fluid around the ligaments and tendons, which was not properly drained; or due to excessive tightening of the ligaments, preventing nutrients from reaching the limb; excessive ointment application; inappropriate movement during recovery; lack of blood in the patient's body, or overall weakness.
The treatment for this involves feeding the patient to strengthen their heart, allowing blood to multiply within them; administering baths; and keeping them entertained with music and laughter.
Then apply wax to the affected limb, as this will draw out nutrients; or continue with an ointment made from thick water, ensuring that nourishment flows and returns to its natural state.
Chapter 22.
On treating twisted bone fractures, where the operation has been incorrectly performed and prevented proper healing.
When the healed limb experiences a recurrence of its twisted and protruding bone fracture, along with knots, which distorts the shape of the limb but does not hinder its natural movement, it is not necessary to assume that the limb has been re-fractured.
There have been many foolish doctors and healers who have done this in our land; such an operation is highly criticized, leading to great fear as its outcome can be fatal.

Chapter 31.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and muscle junction only suffer from dislocation and not any other issues like small displacements or concavities found in other joints.
The hip joint can be dislocated in four ways: inwardly, outwardly, forward, or backward.
And it is most often dislocated in the inner parts, and rarely in the front or back.
A sign of dislocation in the inner parts is when you align the patient's affected leg with their healthy one, it appears longer and the ankle more prominent than the healthy side. The patient cannot bend their foot towards them and the area where they would normally flex their foot becomes swollen due to a trapped swelling, indicating that the head of the dislocation has reached this point.
The sign of dislocation in the outer parts is that the patient's symptoms are opposite to those mentioned above.
The sign of dislocation in the front is when they extend their leg fully but cannot bend it due to pain at the ankle, and if they try to walk, they cannot step on their toes in front; their urine becomes retained, and the back of their knee becomes swollen, requiring them to walk on their heel.
The sign of dislocation in the back is that they cannot extend their ankle and are unable to bend it forward past their knee; their affected leg appears shorter than the other, with a softened knee joint, indicating that the head of the dislocation has reached this area.
